<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;border-top:1px dotted #aaa;">50,000 free civilians dead<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StatsWarCost_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-StatsWarCost-12">[11]</a></sup><br/>
80,000+ slaves dead<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup><br/>
<b>Total: 785,000â1,000,000+ dead</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
</table>

<div id="Theaters_of_the_American_Civil_War" style="font-size:114%;margin:0 4em"><span style="line-height:1.6em"><span class="wrap">Theaters of the <a class="mw-selflink selflink">American Civil War</a></span></span></div>
</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="navbox-list navbox-odd hlist" colspan="2" style="width:100%;padding:0px">
<div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Union_blockade" title="Union blockade">Union blockade</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Eastern_Theater_of_the_American_Civil_War" title="Eastern Theater of the American Civil War">Eastern</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Western_Theater_of_the_American_Civil_War" title="Western Theater of the American Civil War">Western</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lower_Seaboard_Theater_of_the_American_Civil_War" title="Lower Seaboard Theater of the American Civil War">Lower Seaboard</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Trans-Mississippi_Theater_of_the_American_Civil_War" title="Trans-Mississippi Theater of the American Civil War">Trans-Mississippi</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pacific_Coast_Theater_of_the_American_Civil_War" title="Pacific Coast Theater of the American Civil War">Pacific Coast</a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
</div>
<p>The <b>American Civil War</b> was fought in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> from 1861 to 1865. The result of a long-standing <a href="/wiki/Origins_of_the_American_Civil_War" title="Origins of the American Civil War">controversy</a> over <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_United_States" title="Slavery in the United States">slavery</a>, war broke out in April 1861, when <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederates</a> attacked <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Fort_Sumter" title="Battle of Fort Sumter">Fort Sumter</a> in <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a>, shortly after President <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a> was <a href="/wiki/First_inauguration_of_Abraham_Lincoln" title="First inauguration of Abraham Lincoln">inaugurated</a>. The nationalists of the <a href="/wiki/Union_(American_Civil_War)" title="Union (American Civil War)">Union</a> proclaimed loyalty to the U.S. Constitution. They faced <a href="/wiki/Secession_in_the_United_States#Civil_War_era_political_and_legal_views_on_secession" title="Secession in the United States">secessionists</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederate States of America</a>, who advocated for <a href="/wiki/States%27_rights" title="States' rights">states' rights</a> to expand slavery.</p>
<p>Among the 34 <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">U.S. states</a> in February 1861, seven <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">Southern</a> <a href="/wiki/Slave_states_and_free_states" title="Slave states and free states">slave states</a> individually declared their <a href="/wiki/Secession" title="Secession">secession</a> from the U.S. to form the Confederate States of America, or the <i>South.</i> The Confederacy grew to include eleven slave states. The Confederacy was never diplomatically recognized by the <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">United States government</a>, nor was it recognized by any foreign country (although Britain and France granted it <a href="/wiki/Belligerent" title="Belligerent">belligerent status</a>). The states that remained loyal, including the <a href="/wiki/Border_states_(American_Civil_War)" title="Border states (American Civil War)">border states</a> where slavery was legal, were known as the <i>Union</i> or the <i>North</i>.</p>
<p>The North and South quickly raised volunteer and conscription armies that fought mostly in the South over four years. The Union finally won the war when General <a href="/wiki/Robert_E._Lee" title="Robert E. Lee">Robert E. Lee</a> surrendered to General <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Ulysses S. Grant">Ulysses S. Grant</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Appomattox_Court_House" title="Battle of Appomattox Court House">battle of Appomattox</a> followed by a series of <a href="/wiki/Surrender_(military)" title="Surrender (military)">surrenders</a> by Confederate generals throughout the southern states. Four years of intense combat left 620,000 to 750,000 soldiers dead, a higher number than the number of American military deaths in all other wars combined. Much of the South's infrastructure was destroyed, especially the transportation systems, railroads, mills and houses. The Confederacy <a href="/wiki/Conclusion_of_the_American_Civil_War#Capture_of_President_Davis_.28May_10.29" title="Conclusion of the American Civil War">collapsed</a>, slavery <a href="/wiki/Emancipation_Proclamation" title="Emancipation Proclamation">was abolished</a>, and 4 million slaves were freed. The <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_Era" title="Reconstruction Era">Reconstruction Era</a> (1863â1877) overlapped and followed the war, with the process of restoring national unity, strengthening the national government, and granting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_rights" title="Civil rights">civil rights</a> to freed slaves throughout the country. The Civil War is the most studied and <a href="/wiki/Bibliography_of_the_American_Civil_War" title="Bibliography of the American Civil War">written about</a> episode in <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_States" title="History of the United States">American history</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prelude_to_war">Prelude to war</span></h2>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1860" title="United States presidential election, 1860">1860 presidential election</a>, <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republicans</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a>, supported banning slavery in all the <a href="/wiki/Territories_of_the_United_States" title="Territories of the United States">U.S. territories</a>. The Southern states viewed this as a violation of their constitutional rights and as the first step in a grander Republican plan to eventually abolish slavery. The three pro-Union candidates together received an overwhelming 82% majority of the votes cast nationally: Republican Lincoln's votes centered in the north, <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democrat</a> <a href="/wiki/Stephen_A._Douglas" title="Stephen A. Douglas">Stephen A. Douglas</a>' votes were distributed nationally and <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_Union_Party_(United_States)" title="Constitutional Union Party (United States)">Constitutional Unionist</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Bell_(Tennessee_politician)" title="John Bell (Tennessee politician)">John Bell</a>'s votes centered in <a href="/wiki/Tennessee" title="Tennessee">Tennessee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kentucky" title="Kentucky">Kentucky</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>. The Republican Party, dominant in the North, secured a <a href="/wiki/Plurality_(voting)" title="Plurality (voting)">plurality</a> of the popular votes and a majority of the electoral votes nationally, so Lincoln was constitutionally elected president. He was the first Republican Party candidate to win the presidency. However, before <a href="/wiki/First_inauguration_of_Abraham_Lincoln" title="First inauguration of Abraham Lincoln">his inauguration</a>, seven slave states with <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">cotton</a>-based economies declared secession and formed the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederacy</a>. The first six to declare secession had the highest proportions of slaves in their populations, a total of 49 percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup> The first seven with state legislatures to resolve for secession included split majorities for unionists Douglas and Bell in <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a> with 51% and <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a> with 55%. <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a> had voted 46% for those unionists, <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a> with 40%, <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a> with 38%, <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a> with 25%, and <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a> cast <a href="/wiki/Electoral_College_(United_States)" title="Electoral College (United States)">Electoral College</a> votes without a popular vote for president.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup> Of these, only Texas held a referendum on secession.</p>
<p>Eight remaining slave states continued to reject calls for secession. Outgoing Democratic President <a href="/wiki/James_Buchanan" title="James Buchanan">James Buchanan</a> and the incoming Republicans rejected secession as illegal. <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln%27s_first_inaugural_address" title="Abraham Lincoln's first inaugural address">Lincoln's March 4, 1861, inaugural address</a> declared that his administration would not initiate a <a href="/wiki/Civil_war" title="Civil war">civil war</a>. Speaking directly to the "Southern States", he attempted to calm their fears of any threats to slavery, reaffirming, "I have no purpose, directly or indirectly to interfere with the institution of slavery in the United States where it exists. I believe I have no lawful right to do so, and I have no inclination to do so."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> After Confederate forces seized numerous federal forts within territory claimed by the Confederacy, efforts at compromise failed and both sides prepared for war. The Confederates assumed that <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">European</a> countries were so dependent on "<a href="/wiki/King_Cotton" title="King Cotton">King Cotton</a>" that they would intervene, but none did, and none recognized the new Confederate States of America.</p>
<p>Hostilities began on April 12, 1861, when Confederate forces <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Fort_Sumter" title="Battle of Fort Sumter">fired upon Fort Sumter</a>. While in the <a href="/wiki/Western_Theater_of_the_American_Civil_War" title="Western Theater of the American Civil War">Western Theater</a> the Union made significant permanent gains, in the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Theater_of_the_American_Civil_War" title="Eastern Theater of the American Civil War">Eastern Theater</a>, the battle was inconclusive from 1861â1862. Lincoln issued the <a href="/wiki/Emancipation_Proclamation" title="Emancipation Proclamation">Emancipation Proclamation</a>, which made ending slavery a war goal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-proclamation_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-proclamation-20">[19]</a></sup> To the west, by summer 1862 the Union destroyed the Confederate river navy, then much of their western armies, and seized <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a>. The 1863 Union <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Vicksburg" title="Siege of Vicksburg">Siege of Vicksburg</a> split the Confederacy in two at the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a>. In 1863, <a href="/wiki/Robert_E._Lee" title="Robert E. Lee">Robert E. Lee</a>'s Confederate incursion north ended at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gettysburg" title="Battle of Gettysburg">Battle of Gettysburg</a>. Western successes led to <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Ulysses S. Grant">Ulysses S. Grant</a>'s command of all Union armies in 1864. Inflicting an ever-tightening <a href="/wiki/Union_blockade" title="Union blockade">naval blockade</a> of Confederate ports, the Union marshaled the resources and manpower to attack the Confederacy from all directions, leading to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fall_of_Atlanta" title="Fall of Atlanta">fall of Atlanta</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_T._Sherman" title="William T. Sherman">William T. Sherman</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sherman%27s_march_to_the_sea" title="Sherman's march to the sea">his march to the sea</a>. The last significant battles raged around the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Petersburg" title="Siege of Petersburg">Siege of Petersburg</a>. Lee's escape attempt ended with his <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Appomattox_Court_House" title="Battle of Appomattox Court House">surrender at Appomattox Court House</a>, on April 9, 1865. While the military war was coming to an end, the political reintegration of the nation was to take another 12 years, known as the <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_Era" title="Reconstruction Era">Reconstruction Era</a>.</p>
<p>The American Civil War was one of the earliest true <a href="/wiki/Industrial_warfare" title="Industrial warfare">industrial wars</a>. Railroads, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Telegraph" title="Telegraph">telegraph</a>, steamships and <a href="/wiki/Ironclad_warship" title="Ironclad warship">iron-clad</a> ships, and mass-produced weapons were employed extensively. The mobilization of civilian factories, mines, shipyards, banks, transportation and food supplies all foreshadowed the impact of industrialization in <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> and subsequent conflicts. It remains the deadliest war in <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_States" title="History of the United States">American history</a>. From 1861 to 1865, it is estimated that 620,000 to 750,000 soldiers died<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hacker_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-hacker-21">[20]</a></sup>, along with an undetermined number of <a href="/wiki/Civilian_casualties" title="Civilian casualties">civilians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Casualties_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Casualties-23">[b]</a></sup> By one estimate, the war claimed the lives of 10 percent of all Northern males 20â45 years old, and 30 percent of all Southern white males aged 18â40.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuddleston20023_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuddleston20023-24">[22]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes_of_secession">Causes of secession</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Origins_of_the_American_Civil_War" title="Origins of the American Civil War">Origins of the American Civil War</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_events_leading_to_the_American_Civil_War" title="Timeline of events leading to the American Civil War">Timeline of events leading to the American Civil War</a></div>
<p>The causes of secession were complex and have been controversial since the war began, but most academic scholarsÂ identifyÂ slavery as a central cause of the war. James C. Bradford wrote that the issue has been further complicated by <a href="/wiki/Historical_revisionism" title="Historical revisionism">historical revisionists</a>, who have tried to offer a variety of reasons for the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[23]</a></sup> Slavery was the central source of escalating political tension in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1850s_in_the_United_States" title="1850s in the United States">1850s</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican Party</a> was determined to prevent any spread of slavery, and many Southern leaders had threatened secession if the Republican candidate, <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Lincoln</a>, won the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1860_U.S._Presidential_election" title="1860 U.S. Presidential election">1860 election</a>. After Lincoln won many Southern leaders felt that disunion had become their only option, because they thought that they were losing representation, which would hamper their ability to promote pro-slavery acts and policies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Slavery">Slavery</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_Secession_map_1861.svg"><img alt="Map of U.S. showing two kinds of Union states, two phases of secession and territories" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="483" data-file-width="787" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/US_Secession_map_1861.svg/250px-US_Secession_map_1861.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/US_Secession_map_1861.svg/375px-US_Secession_map_1861.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/US_Secession_map_1861.svg/500px-US_Secession_map_1861.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US_Secession_map_1861.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;"><b>Status of the states, 1861</b></div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #A40000; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">Â </span>Â  States that seceded before April 15, 1861</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #EF2929; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">Â </span>Â  States that seceded after April 15, 1861</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #FCE94F; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">Â </span>Â  Union states that permitted slavery</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #204A87; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">Â </span>Â  Union states that banned slavery</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #D3D7CF; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">Â </span>Â  Territories</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Slavery was a major cause of disunion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[26]</a></sup> Although there were opposing views even in the Union States<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[28]</a></sup>, most northern soldiers were largely indifferent on the subject of slavery,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[29]</a></sup> while Confederates fought the war, in large measure, to protect southern society, and slavery was an integral part of it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[30]</a></sup> From the anti-slavery perspective, the issue was primarily about whether the system of slavery was an anachronistic evil that was incompatible with <a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_the_United_States" title="Republicanism in the United States">republicanism</a>. The strategy of the anti-slavery forces was containmentâto stop the expansion and thus put slavery on a path to gradual extinction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeeks2013240_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeeks2013240-33">[31]</a></sup> The slave-holding interests in the South denounced this strategy as infringing upon their Constitutional rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlsen2002237_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlsen2002237-34">[32]</a></sup> Southern whites believed that the emancipation of slaves would destroy the South's economy, due to the large amount of capital invested in slaves and fears of integrating the ex-slave black population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>Slavery was illegal in much of the North, having been outlawed in the late 18th and early 19th centuries. It was also fading in the border states and in Southern cities, but it was expanding in the highly profitable cotton districts of the rural South and Southwest. Subsequent writers on the American Civil War looked to several factors explaining the geographic divide.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sectionalism">Sectionalism</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sectionalism" title="Sectionalism">Sectionalism</a> refers to the different economies, social structure, customs and political values of the North and South.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sectionalism_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-sectionalism-36">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sectionalism17_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-sectionalism17-37">[35]</a></sup> It increased steadily between 1800 and 1860 as the North, which phased slavery out of existence, industrialized, urbanized, and built prosperous farms, while the deep South concentrated on plantation agriculture based on slave labor, together with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subsistence_farming" title="Subsistence farming">subsistence farming</a> for poor <a href="/wiki/Freedman" title="Freedman">freedmen</a>. In the 1840s and 50s, the issue of accepting slavery (in the guise of rejecting slave-owning bishops and missionaries) split the nation's largest religious denominations (the Methodist, Baptist and Presbyterian churches) into separate Northern and Southern denominations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAhlstrom1972648.E2.80.93649_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAhlstrom1972648.E2.80.93649-38">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Historians have debated whether economic differences between the industrial Northeast and the agricultural South helped cause the war. Most historians now disagree with the <a href="/wiki/Economic_determinism" title="Economic determinism">economic determinism</a> of historian <a href="/wiki/Charles_A._Beard" title="Charles A. Beard">Charles A. Beard</a> in the 1920s and emphasize that Northern and Southern economies were largely complementary. While socially different, the sections economically benefited each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-progressive_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-progressive-39">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodworth1996145.2C_151.2C_505.2C_512.2C_554.2C_557.2C_684_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodworth1996145.2C_151.2C_505.2C_512.2C_554.2C_557.2C_684-40">[38]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NewOrleans1841AcrossRiver.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="595" data-file-width="800" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/NewOrleans1841AcrossRiver.jpg/300px-NewOrleans1841AcrossRiver.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/NewOrleans1841AcrossRiver.jpg/450px-NewOrleans1841AcrossRiver.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/NewOrleans1841AcrossRiver.jpg/600px-NewOrleans1841AcrossRiver.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NewOrleans1841AcrossRiver.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/New_Orleans_in_the_American_Civil_War" title="New Orleans in the American Civil War">New Orleans</a>, the largest cotton exporting port for New England and Great Britain textile mills, shipping Mississippi River Valley goods from North, South and Border states</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Protectionism">Protectionism</span></h3>
<p>Historically, southern slave-holding states, because of their low cost manual labor, had little perceived need for mechanization, and supported having the right to sell cotton and purchase manufactured goods from any nation. Northern states, which had heavily invested in their still-nascent manufacturing, could not compete with the full-fledged industries of Europe in offering high prices for cotton imported from the South and low prices for manufactured exports in return. Thus, northern manufacturing interests supported tariffs and protectionism while southern planters demanded free trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThorntonEkelund200421_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThorntonEkelund200421-41">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Democrats in Congress, controlled by Southerners, wrote the tariff laws in the 1830s, 1840s, and 1850s, and kept reducing rates so that the 1857 rates were the lowest since 1816. The Whigs and Republicans complained because they favored high tariffs to stimulate industrial growth, and Republicans called for an increase in tariffs in the 1860 election. The increases were only enacted in 1861 after Southerners resigned their seats in Congress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taussig_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-taussig-42">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHofstadter193850.E2.80.9355_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHofstadter193850.E2.80.9355-43">[41]</a></sup> The tariff issue was and is sometimes citedâlong after the warâby <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lost_Cause" title="Lost Cause">Lost Cause</a> historians and <a href="/wiki/Neo-Confederate" title="Neo-Confederate">neo-Confederate</a> apologists. In 1860â61 none of the groups that proposed compromises to head off secession raised the tariff issue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[42]</a></sup> Pamphleteers North and South rarely mentioned the tariff,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[43]</a></sup> and when some did, for instance, <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Fontaine_Maury" title="Matthew Fontaine Maury">Matthew Fontaine Maury</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[44]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/John_Lothrop_Motley" title="John Lothrop Motley">John Lothrop Motley</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[45]</a></sup> they were generally writing for a foreign audience.</p>
<h3><span id="States'_rights"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="States.27_rights">States' rights</span></h3>
<p>The South argued that each state had the right to secedeâleave the Unionâat any time, that the Constitution was a "compact" or agreement among the states. Northerners (including President Buchanan) rejected that notion as opposed to the will of the Founding Fathers who said they were setting up a perpetual union.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Forrest_McDonald_2002_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Forrest_McDonald_2002-48">[46]</a></sup> Historian James McPherson writes concerning states' rights and other non-slavery explanations:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>While one or more of these interpretations remain popular among the <a href="/wiki/Sons_of_Confederate_Veterans" title="Sons of Confederate Veterans">Sons of Confederate Veterans</a> and other Southern heritage groups, few professional historians now subscribe to them. Of all these interpretations, the states'-rights argument is perhaps the weakest. It fails to ask the question, states' rights for what purpose? States' rights, or sovereignty, was always more a means than an end, an instrument to achieve a certain goal more than a principle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherson20073.E2.80.939_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherson20073.E2.80.939-49">[47]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Territorial_crisis">Territorial crisis</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slave_and_free_states" title="Slave and free states">Slave and free states</a></div>
<p>Between 1803 and 1854, the United States achieved a vast expansion of territory through purchase, negotiation, and conquest. At first, the new states carved out of these territories entering the union were apportioned equally between slave and free states. It was over territories west of the Mississippi that the proslavery and antislavery forces collided.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKrannawitter200849.E2.80.9350_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKrannawitter200849.E2.80.9350-50">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>With the conquest of northern <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> west to <a href="/wiki/Alta_California" title="Alta California">California</a> in 1848, slaveholding interests looked forward to expanding into these lands and perhaps Cuba and Central America as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherson200714_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherson200714-51">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStampp1990190.E2.80.9393_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStampp1990190.E2.80.9393-52">[50]</a></sup> Northern "free soil" interests vigorously sought to curtail any further expansion of slave territory. The <a href="/wiki/Compromise_of_1850" title="Compromise of 1850">Compromise of 1850</a> over California balanced a free soil state with stronger fugitive slave laws for a political settlement after four years of strife in the 1840s. But the states admitted following California were all free: Minnesota (1858), Oregon (1859) and Kansas (1861). In the southern states the question of the territorial expansion of slavery westward again became explosive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherson200713.E2.80.9314_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherson200713.E2.80.9314-53">[51]</a></sup> Both the South and the North drew the same conclusion: "The power to decide the question of slavery for the territories was the power to determine the future of slavery itself."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBestor196419_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBestor196419-54">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherson200716_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherson200716-55">[53]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:298px;max-width:298px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stephen_Arnold_Douglas.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="1379" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Stephen_Arnold_Douglas.jpg/150px-Stephen_Arnold_Douglas.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Stephen_Arnold_Douglas.jpg/225px-Stephen_Arnold_Douglas.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Stephen_Arnold_Douglas.jpg/300px-Stephen_Arnold_Douglas.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Sen. Stephen Douglas, author of the <a href="/wiki/Kansas%E2%80%93Nebraska_Act" title="KansasâNebraska Act">KansasâNebraska Act</a> of 1854</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:142px;max-width:142px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:John_J._Crittenden_-_Brady-Handy.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2057" data-file-width="1326" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/John_J._Crittenden_-_Brady-Handy.jpg/140px-John_J._Crittenden_-_Brady-Handy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/John_J._Crittenden_-_Brady-Handy.jpg/210px-John_J._Crittenden_-_Brady-Handy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/John_J._Crittenden_-_Brady-Handy.jpg/280px-John_J._Crittenden_-_Brady-Handy.jpg 2x" width="140"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Sen. John J. Crittenden, of the 1860 <a href="/wiki/Crittenden_Compromise" title="Crittenden Compromise">Crittenden Compromise</a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By 1860, four doctrines had emerged to answer the question of federal control in the territories, and they all claimed they were sanctioned by the Constitution, implicitly or explicitly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBestor196419.E2.80.9321_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBestor196419.E2.80.9321-56">[54]</a></sup> The first of these "conservative" theories, represented by the <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_Union_Party_(United_States)" title="Constitutional Union Party (United States)">Constitutional Union Party</a>, argued that the <a href="/wiki/Missouri_Compromise" title="Missouri Compromise">Missouri Compromise</a> apportionment of territory north for free soil and south for slavery should become a Constitutional mandate. The <a href="/wiki/Crittenden_Compromise" title="Crittenden Compromise">Crittenden Compromise</a> of 1860 was an expression of this view.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBestor196420_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBestor196420-57">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>The second doctrine of Congressional preeminence, championed by Abraham Lincoln and the Republican Party, insisted that the Constitution did not bind legislators to a policy of balanceâthat slavery could be excluded in a territory as it was done in the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Ordinance" title="Northwest Ordinance">Northwest Ordinance</a> of 1787 at the discretion of Congress,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell1966468.E2.80.9369_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell1966468.E2.80.9369-58">[56]</a></sup> thus Congress could restrict human bondage, but never establish it. The <a href="/wiki/Wilmot_Proviso" title="Wilmot Proviso">Wilmot Proviso</a> announced this position in 1846.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>Senator <a href="/wiki/Stephen_A._Douglas" title="Stephen A. Douglas">Stephen A. Douglas</a> proclaimed the doctrine of territorial or "popular" sovereigntyâwhich asserted that the settlers in a territory had the same rights as states in the Union to establish or disestablish slavery as a purely local matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBestor196421.E2.80.9323_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBestor196421.E2.80.9323-60">[58]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Kansas%E2%80%93Nebraska_Act" title="KansasâNebraska Act">KansasâNebraska Act</a> of 1854 legislated this doctrine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohannsen1973406_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohannsen1973406-61">[59]</a></sup> In Kansas Territory, years of <a href="/wiki/Bleeding_Kansas" title="Bleeding Kansas">pro and anti-slavery violence</a> and political conflict erupted; the congressional House of Representatives voted to admit Kansas as a free state in early 1860, but its admission in the Senate was delayed until January 1861, after the 1860 elections when southern senators began to leave.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Politics_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Politics-62">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>The fourth theory was advocated by Mississippi Senator <a href="/wiki/Jefferson_Davis" title="Jefferson Davis">Jefferson Davis</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBestor196421_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBestor196421-63">[61]</a></sup> one of state sovereignty ("states' rights"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBestor196423_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBestor196423-64">[62]</a></sup> also known as the "Calhoun doctrine",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVaron200858_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVaron200858-65">[63]</a></sup> named after the South Carolinian political theorist and statesman <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Caldwell_Calhoun" title="John Caldwell Calhoun">John C. Calhoun</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell1966470_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell1966470-66">[64]</a></sup> Rejecting the arguments for federal authority or self-government, state sovereignty would empower states to promote the expansion of slavery as part of the Federal Union under the U.S. Constitution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBestor196423.E2.80.9324_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBestor196423.E2.80.9324-67">[65]</a></sup> "States' rights" was an ideology formulated and applied as a means of advancing slave state interests through federal authority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherson20077_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherson20077-68">[66]</a></sup> As historian Thomas L. Krannawitter points out, the "Southern demand for federal slave protection represented a demand for an unprecedented expansion of federal power."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKrannawitter2008232_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKrannawitter2008232-69">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[68]</a></sup> These four doctrines comprised the major ideologies presented to the American public on the matters of slavery, the territories and the U.S. Constitution prior to the 1860 presidential election.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBestor196424.E2.80.9325_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBestor196424.E2.80.9325-71">[69]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="National_elections">National elections</span></h3>
<p>Beginning in the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a> and accelerating after the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>, the people of the United States grew in the sense that their country was a national republic based on the belief that all people had inalienable political liberty and personal rights which could serve as an important example to the rest of the world. Previous regional independence movements such as the <a href="/wiki/Greek_War_of_Independence" title="Greek War of Independence">Greek revolt</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>, the division and redivision of the Latin American political map, and the British-French <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean triumph</a> leading to an interest in redrawing Europe along cultural differences, all conspired to make for a time of upheaval and uncertainty about the basis of the nation-state.</p>
<p>In the world of 19th century self-made Americans, growing in prosperity, population and expanding westward, "freedom" could mean personal liberty or property rights. The unresolved difference would cause failureâfirst in their political institutions, then in their civil life together.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nationalism_and_honor">Nationalism and honor</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Abraham_Lincoln_seated,_Feb_9,_1864.jpg"><img alt="Middle-aged man in a beard posed sitting in a suit, vest and bowtie" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6998" data-file-width="5895" height="261" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Abraham_Lincoln_seated%2C_Feb_9%2C_1864.jpg/220px-Abraham_Lincoln_seated%2C_Feb_9%2C_1864.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Abraham_Lincoln_seated%2C_Feb_9%2C_1864.jpg/330px-Abraham_Lincoln_seated%2C_Feb_9%2C_1864.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Abraham_Lincoln_seated%2C_Feb_9%2C_1864.jpg/440px-Abraham_Lincoln_seated%2C_Feb_9%2C_1864.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Abraham_Lincoln_seated,_Feb_9,_1864.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a><br/>
16th U.S. President (1861â1865)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Nationalism was a powerful force in the early 19th century, with famous spokesmen such as <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Webster" title="Daniel Webster">Daniel Webster</a>. While practically all Northerners supported the Union, Southerners were split between those loyal to the entire United States (called "unionists") and those loyal primarily to the southern region and then the Confederacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPotter1962924.E2.80.9350_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPotter1962924.E2.80.9350-72">[70]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/C._Vann_Woodward" title="C. Vann Woodward">C. Vann Woodward</a> said of the latter group,</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>A great slave societyÂ ... had grown up and miraculously flourished in the heart of a thoroughly bourgeois and partly puritanical republic. It had renounced its bourgeois origins and elaborated and painfully rationalized its institutional, legal, metaphysical, and religious defensesÂ ... When the crisis came it chose to fight. It proved to be the death struggle of a society, which went down in ruins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-counterpoint_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-counterpoint-73">[71]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Perceived insults to Southern collective honor included the enormous popularity of <i><a href="/wiki/Uncle_Tom%27s_Cabin" title="Uncle Tom's Cabin">Uncle Tom's Cabin</a></i> (1852)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-southern_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-southern-74">[72]</a></sup> and the actions of abolitionist <a href="/wiki/John_Brown_(abolitionist)" title="John Brown (abolitionist)">John Brown</a> in trying to incite a <a href="/wiki/John_Brown%27s_raid_on_Harpers_Ferry" title="John Brown's raid on Harpers Ferry">slave rebellion</a> in 1859.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nationalism_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-nationalism-75">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>While the South moved towards a Southern nationalism, leaders in the North were also becoming more nationally minded, and they rejected any notion of splitting the Union. The Republican national electoral platform of 1860 warned that Republicans regarded disunion as <a href="/wiki/Treason" title="Treason">treason</a> and would not tolerate it: "We denounce those threats of disunionÂ ... as denying the vital principles of a free government, and as an avowal of contemplated treason, which it is the imperative duty of an indignant people sternly to rebuke and forever silence."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-republican18_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-republican18-76">[74]</a></sup> The South ignored the warnings: Southerners did not realize how ardently the North would fight to hold the Union together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nationalism19_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-nationalism19-77">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span id="Lincoln's_election"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Lincoln.27s_election">Lincoln's election</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1860" title="United States presidential election, 1860">United States presidential election, 1860</a></div>
<p>The election of Abraham Lincoln in November 1860 was the final trigger for secession.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPotterFehrenbacher1976485_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPotterFehrenbacher1976485-78">[76]</a></sup> Efforts at compromise, including the "<a href="/wiki/Corwin_Amendment" title="Corwin Amendment">Corwin Amendment</a>" and the "<a href="/wiki/Crittenden_Compromise" title="Crittenden Compromise">Crittenden Compromise</a>", failed. Southern leaders feared that Lincoln would stop the expansion of slavery and put it on a course toward extinction. The slave states, which had already become a minority in the House of Representatives, were now facing a future as a perpetual minority in the Senate and Electoral College against an increasingly powerful North. Before Lincoln took office in March 1861, seven slave states had declared their secession and joined to form the Confederacy.</p>
<p>According to Lincoln, the people of the United States had shown that they can be successful in <i>establishing</i> and <i>administering</i> a republic, but a third challenge faced the nation, <i>maintaining</i> the republic, based on the people's vote. The people must now show: ". . . successful maintenance [of the Republic] against a formidable internal attempt to overthrow it. It is now for them to demonstrate to the world that those who can fairly carry an election can also suppress a rebellion; that ballots are the rightful and peaceful successors of bullets; and that when ballots have fairly and constitutionally decided, there can be no successful appeal back to bullets; that there can be no successful appeal, except to ballots themselves, at succeeding elections. Such will be a great lesson of peace; teaching men that what they cannot take by an election, neither can they take it by a war . . .." <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Outbreak_of_the_war">Outbreak of the war</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Secession_crisis">Secession crisis</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charleston_Mercury_Secession_Broadside,_1860.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2997" data-file-width="1530" height="333" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Charleston_Mercury_Secession_Broadside%2C_1860.jpg/170px-Charleston_Mercury_Secession_Broadside%2C_1860.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Charleston_Mercury_Secession_Broadside%2C_1860.jpg/255px-Charleston_Mercury_Secession_Broadside%2C_1860.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Charleston_Mercury_Secession_Broadside%2C_1860.jpg/340px-Charleston_Mercury_Secession_Broadside%2C_1860.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charleston_Mercury_Secession_Broadside,_1860.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The first published imprint of secession, a <a href="/wiki/Broadside_(printing)" title="Broadside (printing)">broadside</a> issued by the <i><a href="/wiki/Charleston_Mercury" title="Charleston Mercury">Charleston Mercury</a></i>, December 20, 1860</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The election of Lincoln caused the legislature of South Carolina to call a state convention to consider secession. Prior to the war, South Carolina did more than any other Southern state to advance the notion that a state had the right to <a href="/wiki/Nullification_(U.S._Constitution)" title="Nullification (U.S. Constitution)">nullify</a> federal laws and, even, secede from the United States. The convention summoned unanimously voted to secede on December 20, 1860, and adopted the "<a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_the_Immediate_Causes_Which_Induce_and_Justify_the_Secession_of_South_Carolina_from_the_Federal_Union" title="Declaration of the Immediate Causes Which Induce and Justify the Secession of South Carolina from the Federal Union">Declaration of the Immediate Causes Which Induce and Justify the Secession of South Carolina from the Federal Union</a>". It argued for states' rights for slave owners in the South, but contained a complaint about states' rights in the North in the form of opposition to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fugitive_Slave_Law_of_1850" title="Fugitive Slave Law of 1850">Fugitive Slave Act</a>, claiming that Northern states were not fulfilling their federal obligations under the Constitution. The "cotton states" of Mississippi, Florida, Alabama, Georgia, Louisiana, and Texas followed suit, seceding in January and February 1861.</p>
<p>Among the ordinances of secession passed by the individual states, those of threeâTexas, Alabama, and Virginiaâspecifically mentioned the plight of the 'slaveholding states' at the hands of northern abolitionists. The rest make no mention of the slavery issue, and are often brief announcements of the dissolution of ties by the legislatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[78]</a></sup> However, at least four statesâSouth Carolina,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[79]</a></sup> Mississippi,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[80]</a></sup> Georgia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[81]</a></sup> and Texas<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thetext_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thetext-84">[82]</a></sup>âalso passed lengthy and detailed explanations of their causes for secession, all of which laid the blame squarely on the movement to abolish slavery and that movement's influence over the politics of the northern states. The southern states believed slaveholding was a constitutional right because of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fugitive_slave_clause" title="Fugitive slave clause">Fugitive slave clause</a> of the Constitution.</p>
<p>These states agreed to form a new federal government, the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederate States of America</a>, on February 4, 1861.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherson198824_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherson198824-85">[83]</a></sup> They took control of federal forts and other properties within their boundaries with little resistance from outgoing President <a href="/wiki/James_Buchanan" title="James Buchanan">James Buchanan</a>, whose term ended on March 4, 1861. Buchanan said that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dred_Scott_decision" title="Dred Scott decision">Dred Scott decision</a> was proof that the South had no reason for secession, and that the Union "...Â was intended to be perpetual," but that "The power by force of arms to compel a State to remain in the Union" was not among the "...Â enumerated powers granted to Congress."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ucsb_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-ucsb-86">[84]</a></sup> One quarter of the U.S. Armyâthe entire garrison in Texasâwas surrendered in February 1861 to state forces by its commanding general, <a href="/wiki/David_E._Twiggs" title="David E. Twiggs">David E. Twiggs</a>, who then joined the Confederacy.</p>
<p>As Southerners resigned their seats in the Senate and the House, Republicans were able to pass bills for projects that had been blocked by Southern Senators before the war, including the <a href="/wiki/Morrill_Tariff" title="Morrill Tariff">Morrill Tariff</a>, land grant colleges (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Morrill_Land-Grant_Colleges_Act" title="Morrill Land-Grant Colleges Act">Morrill Act</a>), a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homestead_Act" title="Homestead Act">Homestead Act</a>, a transcontinental railroad (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacific_Railway_Acts" title="Pacific Railway Acts">Pacific Railway Acts</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WDL_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-WDL-87">[85]</a></sup> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Banking_Act" title="National Banking Act">National Banking Act</a> and the authorization of <a href="/wiki/United_States_Note" title="United States Note">United States Notes</a> by the Legal Tender Act of 1862. The <a href="/wiki/Revenue_Act_of_1861" title="Revenue Act of 1861">Revenue Act of 1861</a> introduced the <a href="/wiki/Income_tax" title="Income tax">income tax</a> to help finance the war.</p>
<p>On December 18, 1860, the <a href="/wiki/Crittenden_Compromise" title="Crittenden Compromise">Crittenden Compromise</a> was proposed to re-establish the <a href="/wiki/Missouri_Compromise" title="Missouri Compromise">Missouri Compromise</a> line by constitutionally banning slavery in territories to the north of the line while guaranteeing it to the south. The adoption of this compromise likely would have prevented the secession of every southern state apart from South Carolina, but Lincoln and the Republicans rejected it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[86]</a></sup> It was then proposed to hold a national referendum on the compromise. The Republicans again rejected the idea, although a majority of both Northerners and Southerners would have voted in favor of it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[87]</a></sup> A pre-war February <a href="/wiki/Peace_Conference_of_1861" title="Peace Conference of 1861">Peace Conference of 1861</a> met in Washington, proposing a solution similar to that of the Crittenden compromise, it was rejected by Congress. The Republicans proposed an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corwin_amendment" title="Corwin amendment">alternative compromise</a> to not interfere with slavery where it existed but the South regarded it as insufficient. Nonetheless, the remaining eight slave states rejected pleas to join the Confederacy following a two-to-one no-vote in Virginia's First Secessionist Convention on April 4, 1861.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherson1988234.E2.80.93266_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherson1988234.E2.80.93266-90">[88]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:President-Jefferson-Davis.jpg"><img alt="Middle-aged man in a goatee posed standing in a suit, vest and bowtie" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2220" data-file-width="1738" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/President-Jefferson-Davis.jpg/170px-President-Jefferson-Davis.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/President-Jefferson-Davis.jpg/255px-President-Jefferson-Davis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/President-Jefferson-Davis.jpg/340px-President-Jefferson-Davis.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:President-Jefferson-Davis.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Jefferson_Davis" title="Jefferson Davis">Jefferson Davis</a>, <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_Confederate_States_of_America" title="President of the Confederate States of America">President of the Confederate States of America</a> (1861â1865)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On March 4, 1861, <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a> was sworn in as President. In his <a href="/wiki/Inauguration" title="Inauguration">inaugural address</a>, he argued that the Constitution was a <i><a href="/wiki/Preamble_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Preamble to the United States Constitution">more perfect union</a></i> than the earlier <a href="/wiki/Articles_of_Confederation" title="Articles of Confederation">Articles of Confederation and Perpetual Union</a>, that it was a binding contract, and called any secession "legally void".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inaugural_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-inaugural-91">[89]</a></sup> He had no intent to invade Southern states, nor did he intend to end slavery where it existed, but said that he would use force to maintain possession of Federal property. The government would make no move to recover post offices, and if resisted, mail delivery would end at state lines. Where popular conditions did not allow peaceful enforcement of Federal law, U.S. Marshals and Judges would be withdrawn. No mention was made of bullion lost from U.S. mints in Louisiana, Georgia and North Carolina. In Lincoln's inaugural address, he stated that it would be U.S. policy to only collect import duties at its ports; there could be no serious injury to the South to justify armed revolution during his administration. His speech closed with a plea for restoration of the bonds of union, famously calling on "the mystic chords of memory" binding the two regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inaugural_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-inaugural-91">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>The South sent delegations to Washington and offered to pay for the federal properties and enter into a peace treaty with the United States. Lincoln rejected any negotiations with Confederate agents because he claimed the Confederacy was not a legitimate government, and that making any treaty with it would be tantamount to recognition of it as a sovereign government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPotterFehrenbacher1976572.E2.80.9373_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPotterFehrenbacher1976572.E2.80.9373-92">[90]</a></sup> Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/William_H._Seward" title="William H. Seward">William Seward</a> who at that time saw himself as the real governor or "prime minister" behind the throne of the inexperienced Lincoln, engaged in unauthorized and indirect negotiations that failed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPotterFehrenbacher1976572.E2.80.9373_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPotterFehrenbacher1976572.E2.80.9373-92">[90]</a></sup> President Lincoln was determined to hold all remaining Union-occupied forts in the Confederacy, <a href="/wiki/Fort_Monroe" title="Fort Monroe">Fort Monroe</a> in Virginia, in Florida, <a href="/wiki/Fort_Pickens" title="Fort Pickens">Fort Pickens</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fort_Jefferson,_Florida" title="Fort Jefferson, Florida">Fort Jefferson</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fort_Zachary_Taylor" title="Fort Zachary Taylor">Fort Taylor</a>, and in the cockpit of secession, Charleston, South Carolina's <a href="/wiki/Fort_Sumter" title="Fort Sumter">Fort Sumter</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Battle_of_Fort_Sumter">Battle of Fort Sumter</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Fort_Sumter" title="Battle of Fort Sumter">Battle of Fort Sumter</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Great_Meeting_Union_Square.jpg"><img alt="Crowd surrounding an equestrian statue topped by a huge U.S. flag" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="887" data-file-width="1349" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Great_Meeting_Union_Square.jpg/300px-Great_Meeting_Union_Square.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Great_Meeting_Union_Square.jpg/450px-Great_Meeting_Union_Square.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Great_Meeting_Union_Square.jpg/600px-Great_Meeting_Union_Square.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Great_Meeting_Union_Square.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mass meeting April 20, 1861, to support the Government at Washington's equestrian statue in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Union_Square_(New_York_City)" title="Union Square (New York City)">Union Square</a> NYC</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Fort Sumter was located in the middle of the harbor of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charleston,_South_Carolina_in_the_American_Civil_War" title="Charleston, South Carolina in the American Civil War">Charleston</a>, South Carolina, where the U.S. fort's garrison had withdrawn to avoid incidents with local militias in the streets of the city. Unlike Buchanan, who allowed commanders to relinquish possession to avoid bloodshed, Lincoln required Maj. Anderson to hold on until fired upon. Jefferson Davis ordered the surrender of the fort. Anderson gave a conditional reply that the Confederate government rejected, and Davis ordered <a href="/wiki/P._G._T._Beauregard" title="P. G. T. Beauregard">P. G. T. Beauregard</a> to attack the fort before a relief expedition could arrive. Troops under Beauregard bombarded Fort Sumter on April 12â13, forcing its capitulation.</p>
<p>The attack on Fort Sumter rallied the North to the defense of American nationalism. Historian <a href="/wiki/Allan_Nevins" title="Allan Nevins">Allan Nevins</a> said:</p>
<dl>
<dd>The thunderclap of Sumter produced a startling crystallization of Northern sentiment.Â ... Anger swept the land. From every side came news of mass meetings, speeches, resolutions, tenders of business support, the muster of companies and regiments, the determined action of governors and legislatures."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[92]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>However, much of the North's attitude was based on the false belief that only a minority of Southerners were actually in favor of secession and that there were large numbers of southern Unionists that could be counted on. Had Northerners realized that most Southerners really did favor secession, they might have hesitated at attempting the enormous task of conquering a united South.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>Lincoln called on all the states to send forces to recapture the fort and other federal properties. With the scale of the rebellion apparently small so far, Lincoln called for only <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/75,000_volunteers" title="75,000 volunteers">75,000 volunteers</a> for 90Â days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherson1988274_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherson1988274-96">[94]</a></sup> The governor of Massachusetts had state regiments on trains headed south the next day. In western Missouri, local secessionists seized <a href="/wiki/Liberty_Arsenal" title="Liberty Arsenal">Liberty Arsenal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[95]</a></sup> On May 3, 1861, Lincoln called for an additional 42,000 volunteers for a period of three years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Proclamation_83_.E2.80.93_Increasing_the_Size_of_the_Army_and_Navy_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Proclamation_83_.E2.80.93_Increasing_the_Size_of_the_Army_and_Navy-98">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>Four states in the middle and upper South had repeatedly rejected Confederate overtures, but now <a href="/wiki/Virginia_in_the_American_Civil_War" title="Virginia in the American Civil War">Virginia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tennessee_in_the_American_Civil_War" title="Tennessee in the American Civil War">Tennessee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arkansas_in_the_American_Civil_War" title="Arkansas in the American Civil War">Arkansas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina_in_the_American_Civil_War" title="North Carolina in the American Civil War">North Carolina</a> refused to send forces against their neighbors, declared their secession, and joined the Confederacy. To reward Virginia, the Confederate capital was moved to <a href="/wiki/Richmond,_Virginia" title="Richmond, Virginia">Richmond</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherson1988276.E2.80.93307_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherson1988276.E2.80.93307-99">[97]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_Secession_map_1863_(BlankMap_derived).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="513" data-file-width="841" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/US_Secession_map_1863_%28BlankMap_derived%29.png/250px-US_Secession_map_1863_%28BlankMap_derived%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/US_Secession_map_1863_%28BlankMap_derived%29.png/375px-US_Secession_map_1863_%28BlankMap_derived%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/US_Secession_map_1863_%28BlankMap_derived%29.png/500px-US_Secession_map_1863_%28BlankMap_derived%29.png 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US_Secession_map_1863_(BlankMap_derived).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
US Secession map 1863. The <a href="/wiki/Union_(American_Civil_War)" title="Union (American Civil War)">Union</a> vs. the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederacy.</a>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #002255; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">Â </span>Â  Union states</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #6895c9; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">Â </span>Â  Union territories not permitting slavery</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ffff00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">Â </span>Â  <a href="/wiki/Border_states_(American_Civil_War)" title="Border states (American Civil War)">Border Union states, permitting slavery</a></div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #dd5500; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">Â </span>Â  Confederate states</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #EDB360; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">Â </span>Â  Union territories permitting slavery (claimed by Confederacy)</div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Attitude_of_the_border_states">Attitude of the border states</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Border_states_(American_Civil_War)" title="Border states (American Civil War)">Border states (American Civil War)</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Delaware" title="Delaware">Delaware</a>, <a href="/wiki/Missouri" title="Missouri">Missouri</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kentucky" title="Kentucky">Kentucky</a> were slave states that were opposed to both secession and coercing the South. <a href="/wiki/West_Virginia" title="West Virginia">West Virginia</a> then joined them as an additional border state after it separated from <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a> and became a state of the <a href="/wiki/Union_(American_Civil_War)" title="Union (American Civil War)">Union</a> in 1863.</p>
<p>Maryland's territory surrounded the United States' capital of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Washington_DC_in_the_Civil_War" title="Washington DC in the Civil War">Washington, DC</a> and could cut it off from the North.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[98]</a></sup> It had numerous anti-Lincoln officials who tolerated anti-army <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_riot_of_1861" title="Baltimore riot of 1861">rioting in Baltimore</a> and the burning of bridges, both aimed at hindering the passage of troops to the South. Maryland's legislature voted overwhelmingly (53â13) to stay in the Union, but also rejected hostilities with its southern neighbors, voting to close Maryland's rail lines to prevent them from being used for war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maryland_State_Archives_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Maryland_State_Archives-101">[99]</a></sup> Lincoln responded by establishing <a href="/wiki/Martial_law" title="Martial law">martial law</a>, and unilaterally suspending <a href="/wiki/Habeas_corpus" title="Habeas corpus">habeas corpus</a>, in Maryland, along with sending in militia units from the North.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherson1988284.E2.80.9387_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherson1988284.E2.80.9387-102">[100]</a></sup> Lincoln rapidly took control of Maryland and the District of Columbia, by seizing many prominent figures, including arresting 1/3 of the members of the <a href="/wiki/Maryland_General_Assembly" title="Maryland General Assembly">Maryland General Assembly</a> on the day it reconvened.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maryland_State_Archives_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-Maryland_State_Archives-101">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[101]</a></sup> All were held without trial, ignoring a ruling by the Chief Justice of the U.S. Supreme Court <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roger_Taney" title="Roger Taney">Roger Taney</a>, a Maryland native, that only Congress (and not the president) could suspend habeas corpus (<a href="/wiki/Ex_parte_Merryman" title="Ex parte Merryman">Ex parte Merryman</a>). Indeed, federal troops imprisoned a prominent Baltimore newspaper editor, <a href="/wiki/Frank_Key_Howard" title="Frank Key Howard">Frank Key Howard</a>, Francis Scott Key's grandson, after he criticized Lincoln in an editorial for ignoring the Supreme Court Chief Justice's ruling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Missouri, an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Missouri_Constitutional_Convention_(1861%E2%80%9363)" title="Missouri Constitutional Convention (1861â63)">elected convention</a> on secession voted decisively to remain within the Union. When pro-Confederate Governor <a href="/wiki/Claiborne_Fox_Jackson" title="Claiborne Fox Jackson">Claiborne F. Jackson</a> called out the state militia, it was attacked by federal forces under General <a href="/wiki/Nathaniel_Lyon" title="Nathaniel Lyon">Nathaniel Lyon</a>, who chased the governor and the rest of the State Guard to the southwestern corner of the state. (<i>See also: <a href="/wiki/Missouri_secession" title="Missouri secession">Missouri secession</a></i>). In the resulting vacuum, the convention on secession reconvened and took power as the Unionist provisional government of Missouri.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nevins_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-nevins-105">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>Kentucky did not secede; for a time, it declared itself neutral. When Confederate forces entered the state in September 1861, neutrality ended and the state reaffirmed its Union status, while trying to maintain slavery. During a brief invasion by Confederate forces, Confederate sympathizers organized a secession convention, inaugurated a governor, and gained recognition from the Confederacy. The rebel government soon went into exile and never controlled Kentucky.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nevins26_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-nevins26-106">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>After Virginia's secession, a <a href="/wiki/Restored_Government_of_Virginia" title="Restored Government of Virginia">Unionist government</a> in <a href="/wiki/Wheeling,_West_Virginia" title="Wheeling, West Virginia">Wheeling</a> asked 48 counties to vote on an ordinance to create a new state on October 24, 1861. A voter turnout of 34 percent approved the statehood bill (96 percent approving).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Convenience_WV_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Convenience_WV-107">[105]</a></sup> The inclusion of 24 secessionist counties<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-copperhead_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-copperhead-108">[106]</a></sup> in the state and the ensuing guerrilla war engaged about 40,000 Federal troops for much of the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherson1988303_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherson1988303-109">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeigley200455_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeigley200455-110">[108]</a></sup> Congress admitted <a href="/wiki/West_Virginia" title="West Virginia">West Virginia</a> to the Union on June 20, 1863. West Virginia provided about 20,000â22,000 soldiers to both the Confederacy and the Union.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-charleston_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-charleston-111">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>A Unionist secession attempt occurred in <a href="/wiki/East_Tennessee" title="East Tennessee">East Tennessee</a>, but was suppressed by the Confederacy, which arrested over 3,000 men suspected of being loyal to the Union. They were held without trial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeely199310.E2.80.9311_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeely199310.E2.80.9311-112">[110]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="War">War</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_American_Civil_War_battles" title="List of American Civil War battles">List of American Civil War battles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Military_leadership_in_the_American_Civil_War" title="Military leadership in the American Civil War">Military leadership in the American Civil War</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:American_Civil_War_Battles_by_Theater,_Year.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="659" data-file-width="1110" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/American_Civil_War_Battles_by_Theater%2C_Year.png/220px-American_Civil_War_Battles_by_Theater%2C_Year.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/American_Civil_War_Battles_by_Theater%2C_Year.png/330px-American_Civil_War_Battles_by_Theater%2C_Year.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/American_Civil_War_Battles_by_Theater%2C_Year.png/440px-American_Civil_War_Battles_by_Theater%2C_Year.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:American_Civil_War_Battles_by_Theater,_Year.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/County_(United_States)" title="County (United States)">County</a> map of Civil War battles by theater and year</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Civil War was a contest marked by the ferocity and frequency of battle. Over four years, 237 named battles were fought, as were many more minor actions and skirmishes, which were often characterized by their bitter intensity and high casualties. In his book <i>The American Civil War</i>, John Keegan writes that "The American Civil War was to prove one of the most ferocious wars ever fought". Without geographic objectives, the only target for each side was the enemy's soldier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[111]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mobilization">Mobilization</span></h3>
<p>As the first seven states began organizing a Confederacy in Montgomery, the entire U.S. army numbered 16,000. However, Northern governors had begun to mobilize their militias.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[112]</a></sup> The Confederate Congress authorized the new nation up to 100,000 troops sent by governors as early as February. By May, Jefferson Davis was pushing for 100,000 men under arms for one year or the duration, and that was answered in kind by the U.S. Congress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the first year of the war, both sides had far more volunteers than they could effectively train and equip. After the initial enthusiasm faded, reliance on the cohort of young men who came of age every year and wanted to join was not enough. Both sides used a draft lawâ<a href="/wiki/Conscription" title="Conscription">conscription</a>âas a device to encourage or force volunteering; relatively few were actually drafted and served. The Confederacy passed a draft law in April 1862 for young men aged 18 to 35; overseers of slaves, government officials, and clergymen were exempt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-questia_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-questia-116">[114]</a></sup> The U.S. Congress followed in July, authorizing a militia draft within a state when it could not meet its quota with volunteers. European <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Immigration_to_the_United_States#Immigration_1790_to_1849" title="History of Immigration to the United States">immigrants</a> joined the <a href="/wiki/Union_Army" title="Union Army">Union Army</a> in large numbers, including 177,000 born in Germany and 144,000 born in Ireland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[115]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:423px;max-width:423px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:237px;max-width:237px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Union_soldiers_entrenched_along_the_west_bank_of_the_Rappahannock_River_at_Fredericksburg,_Virginia_(111-B-157).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="935" data-file-width="1404" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Union_soldiers_entrenched_along_the_west_bank_of_the_Rappahannock_River_at_Fredericksburg%2C_Virginia_%28111-B-157%29.jpg/235px-Union_soldiers_entrenched_along_the_west_bank_of_the_Rappahannock_River_at_Fredericksburg%2C_Virginia_%28111-B-157%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Union_soldiers_entrenched_along_the_west_bank_of_the_Rappahannock_River_at_Fredericksburg%2C_Virginia_%28111-B-157%29.jpg/353px-Union_soldiers_entrenched_along_the_west_bank_of_the_Rappahannock_River_at_Fredericksburg%2C_Virginia_%28111-B-157%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Union_soldiers_entrenched_along_the_west_bank_of_the_Rappahannock_River_at_Fredericksburg%2C_Virginia_%28111-B-157%29.jpg/470px-Union_soldiers_entrenched_along_the_west_bank_of_the_Rappahannock_River_at_Fredericksburg%2C_Virginia_%28111-B-157%29.jpg 2x" width="235"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Union soldiers before Marye's Heights, <a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_Fredericksburg" title="Second Battle of Fredericksburg">Second Fredericksburg</a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:182px;max-width:182px">
