<p>Confederates did not need to invade and hold enemy territory to win, but only needed to fight a defensive war to convince the North that the cost of winning was too high. The North needed to conquer and hold vast stretches of enemy territory and defeat Confederate armies to win.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-confederacy_199-1"><a href="#cite_note-confederacy-199">[197]</a></sup> Lincoln was not a military dictator, and could only continue to fight the war as long as the American public supported a continuation of the war. The Confederacy sought to win independence by out-lasting Lincoln; however, after Atlanta fell and Lincoln defeated McClellan in the election of 1864, all hope for a political victory for the South ended. At that point, Lincoln had secured the support of the Republicans, War Democrats, the border states, emancipated slaves, and the neutrality of Britain and France. By defeating the Democrats and McClellan, he also defeated the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copperheads_(politics)" title="Copperheads (politics)">Copperheads</a> and their peace platform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherson1988771.E2.80.9372_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherson1988771.E2.80.9372-205">[201]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many scholars argue that the Union held an insurmountable long-term advantage over the Confederacy in industrial strength and population. Confederate actions, they argue, only delayed defeat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurrayBernsteinKnox1996235_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurrayBernsteinKnox1996235-206">[202]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeidlerHeidlerColes20021207.E2.80.9310_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeidlerHeidlerColes20021207.E2.80.9310-207">[203]</a></sup> Civil War historian <a href="/wiki/Shelby_Foote" title="Shelby Foote">Shelby Foote</a> expressed this view succinctly: "I think that the North fought that war with one hand behind its backÂ ... If there had been more Southern victories, and a lot more, the North simply would have brought that other hand out from behind its back. I don't think the South ever had a chance to win that War."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWard1990272_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWard1990272-208">[204]</a></sup></p>
<p>A minority view among historians is that the Confederacy lost because, as <a href="/wiki/E._Merton_Coulter" title="E. Merton Coulter">E. Merton Coulter</a> put it, "people did not will hard enough and long enough to win."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[205]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[206]</a></sup> Marxist historian Armstead Robinson agrees, pointing to a class conflict in the Confederates army between the slave owners and the larger number of non-owners. He argues that the non-owner soldiers grew embittered about fighting to preserve slavery, and fought less enthusiastically. He attributes the major Confederate defeats in 1863 at Vicksburg and Missionary Ridge to this class conflict.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[207]</a></sup> However, most historians reject the argument.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[208]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/James_M._McPherson" title="James M. McPherson">James M. McPherson</a>, after reading thousands of letters written by Confederate soldiers, found strong patriotism that continued to the end; they truly believed they were fighting for freedom and liberty. Even as the Confederacy was visibly collapsing in 1864â65, he says most Confederate soldiers were fighting hard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherson1997169.E2.80.9372_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherson1997169.E2.80.9372-213">[209]</a></sup> Historian Gary Gallagher cites General Sherman who in early 1864 commented, "The devils seem to have a determination that cannot but be admired." Despite their loss of slaves and wealth, with starvation looming, Sherman continued, "yet I see no sign of let upâsome few desertersâplenty tired of war, but the masses determined to fight it out."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGallagher199957_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGallagher199957-214">[210]</a></sup></p>
<p>Also important were Lincoln's eloquence in rationalizing the national purpose and his skill in keeping the border states committed to the Union cause. The Emancipation Proclamation was an effective use of the President's war powers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WarLeader_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-WarLeader-215">[211]</a></sup> The Confederate government failed in its attempt to get Europe involved in the war militarily, particularly Britain and France. Southern leaders needed to get European powers to help break up the blockade the Union had created around the Southern ports and cities. Lincoln's naval blockade was 95 percent effective at stopping trade goods; as a result, imports and exports to the South declined significantly. The abundance of European cotton and Britain's hostility to the institution of slavery, along with Lincoln's Atlantic and Gulf of Mexico naval blockades, severely decreased any chance that either Britain or France would enter the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherson1988382.E2.80.9388_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherson1988382.E2.80.9388-216">[212]</a></sup></p>
<p>Historian Don Doyle has argued that the Union victory had a major impact on the course of world history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[213]</a></sup> The Union victory energized popular democratic forces. A Confederate victory, on the other hand, would have meant a new birth of slavery, not freedom. Historian Fergus Bordewich, following Doyle, argues that:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The North's victory decisively proved the durability of democratic government. Confederate independence, on the other hand, would have established an American model for reactionary politics and race-based repression that would likely have cast an international shadow into the twentieth century and perhaps beyond."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[214]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Scholars have debated what the effects of the war were on political and economic power in the South.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-219">[215]</a></sup> The prevailing view is that the southern planter elite retained its powerful position in the South.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_219-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-219">[215]</a></sup> However, a 2017 study challenges this, noting that while some Southern elites retained their economic status, the turmoil of the 1860s created greater opportunities for economic mobility in the South than in the North.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_219-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-219">[215]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Costs">Costs</span></h3>
<p>The war resulted in at least 1,030,000 casualties (3 percent of the population), including about 620,000 soldier deathsâtwo-thirds by disease, and 50,000 civilians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StatsWarCost_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-StatsWarCost-12">[11]</a></sup> Binghamton University historian J. David Hacker believes the number of soldier deaths was approximately 750,000, 20 percent higher than traditionally estimated, and possibly as high as 850,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hacker_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-hacker-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-recounting_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-recounting-220">[216]</a></sup> The war accounted for more American deaths than in all other U.S. wars combined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherson1988xix_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherson1988xix-221">[217]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:457px;max-width:457px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:112px;max-width:112px">

</div>

<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:187px;max-width:187px">

</div>

<div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">National cemetery in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andersonville_National_Cemetery" title="Andersonville National Cemetery">Andersonville, GA.</a></div>

<p>Based on 1860 census figures, 8 percent of all white males aged 13 to 43 died in the war, including 6 percent in the North and 18 percent in the South.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVinovskis19907_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVinovskis19907-222">[218]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slate_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-slate-223">[219]</a></sup> About 56,000 soldiers <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War_prison_camps" title="American Civil War prison camps">died in prison camps</a> during the War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nationalgeographic_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-nationalgeographic-224">[220]</a></sup> An estimated 60,000 men lost limbs in the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT_TR_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT_TR-225">[221]</a></sup></p>
<p>Union army dead, amounting to 15 percent of the over two million who served, was broken down as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-civilwarhome.com_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-civilwarhome.com-7">[6]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>110,070 killed in action (67,000) or died of wounds (43,000).</li>
<li>199,790 died of disease (75 percent was due to the war, the remainder would have occurred in civilian life anyway)</li>
<li>24,866 died in Confederate prison camps</li>
<li>9,058 killed by accidents or drowning</li>
<li>15,741 other/unknown deaths</li>
<li>359,528 total dead</li>
</ul>
<p>In addition there were 4,523 deaths in the Navy (2,112 in battle) and 460 in the Marines (148 in battle).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Official_DOD_data_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-Official_DOD_data-8">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>Black troops made up 10 percent of the Union death toll, they amounted to 15 percent of disease deaths but less than 3 percent of those killed in battle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-civilwarhome.com_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-civilwarhome.com-7">[6]</a></sup> Losses among <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">African Americans</a> were high, in the last year and a half and from all reported casualties, approximately 20 percent of all African Americans enrolled in the military lost their lives during the Civil War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aptheker_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aptheker-226">[222]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:16</sup> Notably, their mortality rate was significantly higher than white soldiers:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>[We] find, according to the revised official data, that of the slightly over two millions troops in the United States Volunteers, over 316,000 died (from all causes), or 15.2 percent. Of the 67,000 Regular Army (white) troops, 8.6 percent, or not quite 6,000, died. Of the approximately 180,000 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Colored_Troops" title="United States Colored Troops">United States Colored Troops</a>, however, over 36,000 died, or 20.5 percent. In other words, the mortality <i>"rate"</i> amongst the United States Colored Troops in the Civil War was thirty-five percent greater than that among other troops, notwithstanding the fact that the former were not enrolled until some eighteen months after the fighting began.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aptheker_226-1"><a href="#cite_note-Aptheker-226">[222]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:16</sup></p>
</blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Burial_of_the_dead_on_the_Antietam_battlefield_army.mil-2008-09-10-145638.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2487" data-file-width="4152" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Burial_of_the_dead_on_the_Antietam_battlefield_army.mil-2008-09-10-145638.jpg/230px-Burial_of_the_dead_on_the_Antietam_battlefield_army.mil-2008-09-10-145638.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Burial_of_the_dead_on_the_Antietam_battlefield_army.mil-2008-09-10-145638.jpg/345px-Burial_of_the_dead_on_the_Antietam_battlefield_army.mil-2008-09-10-145638.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Burial_of_the_dead_on_the_Antietam_battlefield_army.mil-2008-09-10-145638.jpg/460px-Burial_of_the_dead_on_the_Antietam_battlefield_army.mil-2008-09-10-145638.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Burial_of_the_dead_on_the_Antietam_battlefield_army.mil-2008-09-10-145638.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Burying Union dead on the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Antietam" title="Battle of Antietam">Antietam</a> battlefield, 1862</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Confederate records compiled by historian William F. Fox list 74,524 killed and died of wounds and 59,292 died of disease. Including Confederate estimates of battle losses where no records exist would bring the Confederate death toll to 94,000 killed and died of wounds. Fox complained, however, that records were incomplete, especially during the last year of the war, and that battlefield reports likely under-counted deaths (many men counted as wounded in battlefield reports subsequently died of their wounds). Thomas L. Livermore, using Fox's data, put the number of Confederate non-combat deaths at 166,000, using the official estimate of Union deaths from disease and accidents and a comparison of Union and Confederate enlistment records, for a total of 260,000 deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-civilwarhome.com_7-5"><a href="#cite_note-civilwarhome.com-7">[6]</a></sup> However, this excludes the 30,000 deaths of Confederate troops in prisons, which would raise the minimum number of deaths to 290,000.</p>
<p>The United States National Park Service uses the following figures in its official tally of war losses:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nps.gov_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-nps.gov-3">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>Union: 853,838</b></p>
<ul>
<li>110,100 killed in action</li>
<li>224,580 disease deaths</li>
<li>275,154 wounded in action</li>
<li>211,411 captured (including 30,192 who died as POWs)</li>
</ul>
<p><b>Confederate: 914,660</b></p>
<ul>
<li>94,000 killed in action</li>
<li>164,000 disease deaths</li>
<li>194,026 wounded in action</li>
<li>462,634 captured (including 31,000 who died as POWs)</li>
</ul>
<p>While the figures of 360,000 army deaths for the Union and 260,000 for the Confederacy remained commonly cited, they are incomplete. In addition to many Confederate records being missing, partly as a result of Confederate widows not reporting deaths due to being ineligible for benefits, both armies only counted troops who died during their service, and not the tens of thousands who died of wounds or diseases after being discharged. This often happened only a few days or weeks later. <a href="/wiki/Francis_Amasa_Walker" title="Francis Amasa Walker">Francis Amasa Walker</a>, Superintendent of the 1870 Census, used census and Surgeon General data to estimate a minimum of 500,000 Union military deaths and 350,000 Confederate military deaths, for a total death toll of 850,000 soldiers. While Walker's estimates were originally dismissed because of the 1870 Census's undercounting, it was later found that the census was only off by 6.5%, and that the data Walker used would be roughly accurate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-recounting_220-1"><a href="#cite_note-recounting-220">[216]</a></sup></p>
<p>Analyzing the number of dead by using census data to calculate the deviation of the death rate of men of fighting age from the norm suggests that at least 627,000 and at most 888,000, but most likely 761,000 soldiers, died in the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHacker2011307.E2.80.9348_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHacker2011307.E2.80.9348-22">[21]</a></sup> This would break down to approximately 350,000 Confederate and 411,000 Union military deaths, going by the proportion of Union to Confederate battle losses.</p>
<p>Deaths among former slaves has proven much harder to estimate, due to the lack of reliable census data at the time, though they were known to be considerable, as former slaves were set free or escaped in massive numbers in an area where the Union army did not have sufficient shelter, doctors, or food for them. University of Connecticut Professor James Downs states that tens to hundreds of thousands of slaves died during the war from disease, starvation, exposure, or execution at the hands of the Confederates, and that if these deaths are counted in the war's total, the death toll would exceed 1 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[223]</a></sup></p>
<p>Losses were far higher than during the recent <a href="/wiki/Mexican%E2%80%93American_War" title="MexicanâAmerican War">defeat of Mexico</a>, which saw roughly thirteen thousand American deaths, including fewer than two thousand killed in battle, between 1846 and 1848. One reason for the high number of battle deaths during the war was the continued use of tactics similar to those of the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a> at the turn of the century, such as <a href="/wiki/Charge_(warfare)" title="Charge (warfare)">charging</a>. With the advent of more accurate rifled barrels, <a href="/wiki/Mini%C3%A9_ball" title="MiniÃ© ball">MiniÃ© balls</a> and (near the end of the war for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Union_army" title="Union army">Union army</a>) repeating firearms such as the <a href="/wiki/Spencer_repeating_rifle" title="Spencer repeating rifle">Spencer Repeating Rifle</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Henry_rifle" title="Henry rifle">Henry Repeating Rifle</a>, soldiers were mowed down when standing in lines in the open. This led to the adoption of <a href="/wiki/Trench_warfare" title="Trench warfare">trench warfare</a>, a style of fighting that defined much of World War I.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[224]</a></sup></p>
<p>The wealth amassed in slaves and slavery for the Confederacy's 3.5 million blacks effectively ended when Union armies arrived; they were nearly all freed by the Emancipation Proclamation. Slaves in the border states and those located in some former Confederate territory occupied before the Emancipation Proclamation were freed by state action or (on December 6, 1865) by the <a href="/wiki/Thirteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Thirteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Thirteenth Amendment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[225]</a></sup></p>
<p>The war destroyed much of the wealth that had existed in the South. All accumulated investment Confederate bonds was forfeit; most banks and railroads were bankrupt. Income per person in the South dropped to less than 40 percent of that of the North, a condition that lasted until well into the 20th century. Southern influence in the U.S. federal government, previously considerable, was greatly diminished until the latter half of the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economist_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-economist-230">[226]</a></sup> The full restoration of the Union was the work of a highly contentious postwar era known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reconstruction_Era_of_the_United_States" title="Reconstruction Era of the United States">Reconstruction</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emancipation">Emancipation</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Slavery_as_a_war_issue">Slavery as a war issue</span></h4>
<p>While not all Southerners saw themselves as fighting to preserve slavery, most of the officers and over a third of the rank and file in <a href="/wiki/Robert_E._Lee" title="Robert E. Lee">Lee</a>'s army had close family ties to slavery. To Northerners, in contrast, the motivation was primarily to preserve the <a href="/wiki/Union_(American_Civil_War)" title="Union (American Civil War)">Union</a>, not to abolish slavery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFoner1981.3F_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoner1981.3F-231">[227]</a></sup> Abraham Lincoln consistently made preserving the Union the central goal of the war, though he increasingly saw slavery as a crucial issue and made ending it an additional goal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFoner201074_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoner201074-232">[228]</a></sup> Lincoln's decision to issue the <a href="/wiki/Emancipation_Proclamation" title="Emancipation Proclamation">Emancipation Proclamation</a> angered both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peace_Democrats" title="Peace Democrats">Peace Democrats</a> ("Copperheads") and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/War_Democrats" title="War Democrats">War Democrats</a>, but energized most Republicans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[229]</a></sup> By warning that free blacks would flood the North, Democrats made gains in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1862_congressional_elections" title="1862 congressional elections">1862 elections</a>, but they did not gain control of Congress. The Republicans' counterargument that slavery was the mainstay of the enemy steadily gained support, with the Democrats losing decisively in the 1863 elections in the northern state of Ohio when they tried to resurrect anti-black sentiment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[230]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Emancipation_Proclamation">Emancipation Proclamation</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Emancipation_Proclamation" title="Emancipation Proclamation">Emancipation Proclamation</a></div>
<p>The Emancipation Proclamation enabled African-Americans, both free blacks and escaped slaves, to join the Union Army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BlacksUSATroops_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-BlacksUSATroops-237">[e]</a></sup> About 190,000 volunteered, further enhancing the numerical advantage the Union armies enjoyed over the Confederates, who did not dare emulate the equivalent manpower source for fear of fundamentally undermining the legitimacy of slavery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SlavesinConfedArmy_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-SlavesinConfedArmy-241">[f]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the Civil War, sentiment concerning slaves, enslavement and emancipation in the United States was divided. In 1861, Lincoln worried that premature attempts at emancipation would mean the loss of the border states, and that "to lose Kentucky is nearly the same as to lose the whole game."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[236]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copperheads_(politics)" title="Copperheads (politics)">Copperheads</a> and some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/War_Democrats" title="War Democrats">War Democrats</a> opposed emancipation, although the latter eventually accepted it as part of <a href="/wiki/Total_war" title="Total war">total war</a> needed to save the Union.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[237]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:448px;max-width:448px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Contrabands_at_Headquarters_of_General_Lafayette_by_Mathew_Brady.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="782" data-file-width="1245" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Contrabands_at_Headquarters_of_General_Lafayette_by_Mathew_Brady.jpg/300px-Contrabands_at_Headquarters_of_General_Lafayette_by_Mathew_Brady.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Contrabands_at_Headquarters_of_General_Lafayette_by_Mathew_Brady.jpg/450px-Contrabands_at_Headquarters_of_General_Lafayette_by_Mathew_Brady.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Contrabands_at_Headquarters_of_General_Lafayette_by_Mathew_Brady.jpg/600px-Contrabands_at_Headquarters_of_General_Lafayette_by_Mathew_Brady.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:142px;max-width:142px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Soldiers_White_Black_1861.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="711" data-file-width="520" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Soldiers_White_Black_1861.jpg/140px-Soldiers_White_Black_1861.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Soldiers_White_Black_1861.jpg/210px-Soldiers_White_Black_1861.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Soldiers_White_Black_1861.jpg/280px-Soldiers_White_Black_1861.jpg 2x" width="140"/></a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">
<div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">Left: <a href="/wiki/Contraband_(American_Civil_War)" title="Contraband (American Civil War)">Contrabands</a>âfugitive slavesâcooks, laundresses, laborers, teamsters, railroad repair crewsâfled to the Union Army, but were not officially freed until 1863. <a href="/wiki/Emancipation_Proclamation" title="Emancipation Proclamation">Emancipation Proclamation</a></div>
.
<div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">Right: In 1863, the Union army accepted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freedmen" title="Freedmen">Freedmen</a>. Seen here are Black and White teen-aged soldiers.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At first, Lincoln reversed attempts at emancipation by Secretary of War <a href="/wiki/Simon_Cameron" title="Simon Cameron">Simon Cameron</a> and Generals <a href="/wiki/John_C._Fr%C3%A9mont" title="John C. FrÃ©mont">John C. FrÃ©mont</a> (in Missouri) and <a href="/wiki/David_Hunter" title="David Hunter">David Hunter</a> (in South Carolina, Georgia and Florida) to keep the loyalty of the border states and the War Democrats. Lincoln warned the border states that a more radical type of emancipation would happen if his gradual plan based on compensated emancipation and voluntary colonization was rejected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[238]</a></sup> But only the District of Columbia accepted Lincoln's gradual plan, which was enacted by Congress. When Lincoln told his cabinet about his proposed emancipation proclamation, Seward advised Lincoln to wait for a victory before issuing it, as to do otherwise would seem like "our last shriek on the retreat".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[239]</a></sup> Lincoln laid the groundwork for public support in an open letter published in abolitionist Horace Greeley's newspaper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[240]</a></sup></p>
<p>In September 1862, the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Antietam" title="Battle of Antietam">Battle of Antietam</a> provided this opportunity, and the subsequent <a href="/wiki/War_Governors%27_Conference" title="War Governors' Conference">War Governors' Conference</a> added support for the proclamation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[241]</a></sup> Lincoln issued his preliminary <a href="/wiki/Emancipation_Proclamation" title="Emancipation Proclamation">Emancipation Proclamation</a> on September 22, 1862, and his final Emancipation Proclamation on January 1, 1863. In his letter to <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Albert_G._Hodges&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Albert G. Hodges (page does not exist)">Albert G. Hodges</a>, Lincoln explained his belief that "If slavery is not wrong, nothing is wrongÂ ... And yet I have never understood that the Presidency conferred upon me an unrestricted right to act officially upon this judgment and feelingÂ ... I claim not to have controlled events, but confess plainly that events have controlled me."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[242]</a></sup></p>
<p>Lincoln's moderate approach succeeded in inducing border states, War Democrats and emancipated slaves to fight for the Union. The Union-controlled border states (Kentucky, Missouri, Maryland, Delaware and West Virginia) and Union-controlled regions around New Orleans, Norfolk and elsewhere, were not covered by the Emancipation Proclamation. All abolished slavery on their own, except Kentucky and Delaware.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[243]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since the Emancipation Proclamation was based on the President's war powers, it only included territory held by Confederates at the time. However, the Proclamation became a symbol of the Union's growing commitment to add emancipation to the Union's definition of liberty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[244]</a></sup> The Emancipation Proclamation greatly reduced the Confederacy's hope of getting aid from Britain or France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsanteMazama200482_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsanteMazama200482-251">[245]</a></sup> By late 1864, Lincoln was playing a leading role in getting Congress to vote for the <a href="/wiki/Thirteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Thirteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Thirteenth Amendment</a>, which made emancipation universal and permanent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHolzerGabbard2007172.E2.80.93174_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHolzerGabbard2007172.E2.80.93174-252">[246]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Texas_v._White">Texas v. White</span></h3>
<p>In <i><a href="/wiki/Texas_v._White" title="Texas v. White">Texas v. White</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Supreme_Court_cases,_volume_74" title="List of United States Supreme Court cases, volume 74">74</a> <a href="/wiki/United_States_Reports" title="United States Reports">U.S.</a> <a class="external text" href="https://supreme.justia.com/cases/federal/us/74/700/" rel="nofollow">700</a> (1869) the United States Supreme Court ruled that Texas had remained a state ever since it first joined the Union, despite claims that it joined the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederate States</a>; the court further held that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_United_States" title="Constitution of the United States">Constitution</a> did not permit <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">states</a> to unilaterally <a href="/wiki/Secession" title="Secession">secede</a> from the United States, and that the ordinances of secession, and all the acts of the legislatures within seceding states intended to give effect to such ordinances, were "absolutely <a href="/wiki/Void_(law)" title="Void (law)">null</a>", under the constitution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[247]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reconstruction">Reconstruction</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reconstruction_Era_of_the_United_States" title="Reconstruction Era of the United States">Reconstruction Era of the United States</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Freedmen_richmond_sewing_women.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="493" data-file-width="640" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Freedmen_richmond_sewing_women.jpg/220px-Freedmen_richmond_sewing_women.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Freedmen_richmond_sewing_women.jpg/330px-Freedmen_richmond_sewing_women.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Freedmen_richmond_sewing_women.jpg/440px-Freedmen_richmond_sewing_women.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Freedmen_richmond_sewing_women.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Northern teachers traveled into the South to provide education and training for the newly freed population.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Reconstruction began during the war, with the Emancipation Proclamation of January 1, 1863, and it continued until 1877.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[248]</a></sup> It comprised multiple complex methods to resolve the outstanding issues of the war's aftermath, the most important of which were the three "<a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_Amendments" title="Reconstruction Amendments">Reconstruction Amendments</a>" to the Constitution, which remain in effect to the present time: the 13th (1865), the 14th (1868) and the 15th (1870). From the Union perspective, the goals of Reconstruction were to consolidate the Union victory on the battlefield by reuniting the Union; to guarantee a "<a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_the_United_States" title="Republicanism in the United States">republican form of government</a> for the ex-Confederate states; and to permanently end slaveryâand prevent semi-slavery status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[249]</a></sup></p>
<p>President Johnson took a lenient approach and saw the achievement of the main war goals as realized in 1865, when each ex-rebel state repudiated secession and ratified the Thirteenth Amendment. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radical_Republicans" title="Radical Republicans">Radical Republicans</a> demanded proof that Confederate nationalism was dead and that the slaves were truly free. They came to the fore after the 1866 elections and undid much of Johnson's work. In 1872 the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Liberal Republican Party (United States)">"Liberal Republicans"</a> argued that the war goals had been achieved and that Reconstruction should end. They ran a presidential ticket in 1872 but were decisively defeated. In 1874, Democrats, primarily Southern, took control of Congress and opposed any more reconstruction. The <a href="/wiki/Compromise_of_1877" title="Compromise of 1877">Compromise of 1877</a> closed with a national consensus that the Civil War had finally ended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[250]</a></sup> With the withdrawal of federal troops, however, whites retook control of every Southern legislature; the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jim_Crow" title="Jim Crow">Jim Crow</a> period of disenfranchisement and legal segregation was about to begin.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Memory_and_historiography">Memory and historiography</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:434px;max-width:434px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Grand_Army_of_the_Republic_by_Swatjester.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="677" data-file-width="1024" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Grand_Army_of_the_Republic_by_Swatjester.jpg/200px-Grand_Army_of_the_Republic_by_Swatjester.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Grand_Army_of_the_Republic_by_Swatjester.jpg/300px-Grand_Army_of_the_Republic_by_Swatjester.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Grand_Army_of_the_Republic_by_Swatjester.jpg/400px-Grand_Army_of_the_Republic_by_Swatjester.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:228px;max-width:228px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cherokee_Confederates_Reunion.gif"><img alt="" data-file-height="353" data-file-width="604" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Cherokee_Confederates_Reunion.gif/226px-Cherokee_Confederates_Reunion.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Cherokee_Confederates_Reunion.gif/339px-Cherokee_Confederates_Reunion.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Cherokee_Confederates_Reunion.gif/452px-Cherokee_Confederates_Reunion.gif 2x" width="226"/></a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">
<div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">Left: Monument to the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Army_of_the_Republic" title="Grand Army of the Republic">Grand Army of the Republic</a>, a Union veteran organization</div>
<div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">Right: <a href="/wiki/Cherokee_in_the_American_Civil_War" title="Cherokee in the American Civil War">Cherokee Confederates</a> reunion in New Orleans, 1903</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Civil War is one of the central events in American collective memory. There are innumerable statues, commemorations, books and archival collections. The memory includes the home front, military affairs, the treatment of soldiers, both living and dead, in the war's aftermath, depictions of the war in literature and art, evaluations of heroes and villains, and considerations of the moral and political lessons of the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-controversy_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-controversy-257">[251]</a></sup> The last theme includes moral evaluations of <a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">racism</a> and slavery, heroism in combat and heroism behind the lines, and the issues of democracy and minority rights, as well as the notion of an "<a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Liberty" title="Empire of Liberty">Empire of Liberty</a>" influencing the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-american83_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-american83-258">[252]</a></sup></p>
<p>Professional historians have paid much more attention to the causes of the war, than to the war itself. Military history has largely developed outside academe, leading to a proliferation of solid studies by non-scholars who are thoroughly familiar with the primary sources, pay close attention to battles and campaigns, and write for the large public readership, rather than the small scholarly community. <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Catton" title="Bruce Catton">Bruce Catton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shelby_Foote" title="Shelby Foote">Shelby Foote</a> are among the best-known writers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[253]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[254]</a></sup> Practically every major figure in the war, both North and South, has had a serious biographical study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[255]</a></sup> Deeply religious Southerners saw the hand of God in history, which demonstrated His wrath at their sinfulness, or His rewards for their suffering. Historian Wilson Fallin has examined the sermons of white and black <a href="/wiki/Baptists" title="Baptists">Baptist</a> preachers after the War. Southern white preachers said:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>God had chastised them and given them a special missionâto maintain orthodoxy, strict biblicism, personal piety, and traditional race relations. Slavery, they insisted, had not been sinful. Rather, emancipation was a historical tragedy and the end of Reconstruction was a clear sign of God's favor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[256]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>In sharp contrast, Black preachers interpreted the Civil War as:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>God's gift of freedom. They appreciated opportunities to exercise their independence, to worship in their own way, to affirm their worth and dignity, and to proclaim the fatherhood of God and the brotherhood of man. Most of all, they could form their own churches, associations, and conventions. These institutions offered self-help and racial uplift, and provided places where the gospel of liberation could be proclaimed. As a result, black preachers continued to insist that God would protect and help him; God would be their rock in a stormy land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[257]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lost_Cause">Lost Cause</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lost_Cause_of_the_Confederacy" title="Lost Cause of the Confederacy">Lost Cause of the Confederacy</a></div>
<p>Memory of the war in the white South crystallized in the myth of the <a href="/wiki/Lost_Cause_of_the_Confederacy" title="Lost Cause of the Confederacy">"Lost Cause"</a>, shaping regional identity and race relations for generations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-confederacy84_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-confederacy84-264">[258]</a></sup> Alan T. Nolan notes that the Lost Cause was expressly "a rationalization, a cover-up to vindicate the name and fame" of those in rebellion. Some claims revolve around the insignificance of slavery; some appeals highlight cultural differences between North and South; the military conflict by Confederate actors is idealized; in any case, secession was said to be lawful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[259]</a></sup> Nolan argues that the adoption of the Lost Cause perspective facilitated the reunification of the North and the South while excusing the "virulent racism" of the 19th century, sacrificing African-American progress to a white man's reunification. He also deems the Lost Cause "a caricature of the truth. This caricature wholly misrepresents and distorts the facts of the matter" in every instance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266">[260]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Civil_War_centennial_issues.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1617" data-file-width="2598" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Civil_War_centennial_issues.jpg/300px-Civil_War_centennial_issues.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Civil_War_centennial_issues.jpg/450px-Civil_War_centennial_issues.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Civil_War_centennial_issues.jpg/600px-Civil_War_centennial_issues.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Civil_War_centennial_issues.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Beginning in 1961 the U.S. Post Office released <a href="/wiki/Commemorative_stamp" title="Commemorative stamp">Commemorative stamps</a> for five famous battles, each issued on the 100th anniversary of the respective battle.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Beardian_historiography">Beardian historiography</span></h3>
<p>The interpretation of the Civil War presented by <a href="/wiki/Charles_A._Beard" title="Charles A. Beard">Charles A. Beard</a> and Mary R. Beard in <i>The Rise of American Civilization</i> (1927) was highly influential among historians and the general public until the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Movement" title="Civil Rights Movement">Civil Rights Movement</a> of the 1950s and 1960s. The Beards downplayed slavery, abolitionism, and issues of morality. They ignored constitutional issues of states' rights and even ignored American nationalism as the force that finally led to victory in the war. Indeed, the ferocious combat itself was passed over as merely an ephemeral event. Much more important was the calculus of class conflict. The Beards announced that the Civil War was really:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>[A] social cataclysm in which the capitalists, laborers, and farmers of the North and West drove from power in the national government the planting aristocracy of the South.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267">[261]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The Beards themselves abandoned their interpretation by the 1940s and it became defunct among historians in the 1950s, when scholars shifted to an emphasis on slavery. However, Beardian themes still echo among Lost Cause writers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[262]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_War_commemoration">Civil War commemoration</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Commemoration_of_the_American_Civil_War" title="Commemoration of the American Civil War">Commemoration of the American Civil War</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Commemoration_of_the_American_Civil_War_on_postage_stamps" title="Commemoration of the American Civil War on postage stamps">Commemoration of the American Civil War on postage stamps</a></div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:448px;max-width:448px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Grand_Army_of_the_Republic_issue_1948_3c.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="761" data-file-width="1181" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Grand_Army_of_the_Republic_issue_1948_3c.JPG/220px-Grand_Army_of_the_Republic_issue_1948_3c.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Grand_Army_of_the_Republic_issue_1948_3c.JPG/330px-Grand_Army_of_the_Republic_issue_1948_3c.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Grand_Army_of_the_Republic_issue_1948_3c.JPG/440px-Grand_Army_of_the_Republic_issue_1948_3c.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:United_Confederate_Veterans_1951_3c.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="677" data-file-width="1046" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/United_Confederate_Veterans_1951_3c.JPG/220px-United_Confederate_Veterans_1951_3c.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/United_Confederate_Veterans_1951_3c.JPG/330px-United_Confederate_Veterans_1951_3c.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/United_Confederate_Veterans_1951_3c.JPG/440px-United_Confederate_Veterans_1951_3c.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">
<div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">Left: Grand Army of the Republic (Union)</div>
<div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">Right: United Confederate Veterans</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The American Civil War has been commemorated in many capacities ranging from the reenactment of battles, to statues and memorial halls erected, to films being produced, to stamps and coins with Civil War themes being issued, all of which helped to shape public memory. This varied advent occurred in greater proportions on the 100th and 150th anniversary. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gallagher_269-0"><a href="#cite_note-gallagher-269">[263]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_the_United_States" title="Cinema of the United States">Hollywood</a>'s take on the war has been especially influential in shaping public memory, as seen in such film classics as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birth_of_a_Nation" title="Birth of a Nation">Birth of a Nation</a></i> (1915), <i><a href="/wiki/Gone_with_the_Wind_(film)" title="Gone with the Wind (film)">Gone with the Wind</a></i> (1939), and more recently <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lincoln_(2012_film)" title="Lincoln (2012 film)">Lincoln</a></i> (2012). <a href="/wiki/Ken_Burns" title="Ken Burns">Ken Burns</a> produced a notable PBS series on television titled <i><a href="/wiki/The_Civil_War_(TV_series)" title="The Civil War (TV series)">The Civil War</a></i> (1990). It was digitally remastered and re-released in 2015.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Technological_significance">Technological significance</span></h3>
<p>There were numerous technological innovations during the Civil War that had a great impact on 19th century science. The Civil War was one of the earliest examples of an "<a href="/wiki/Industrial_warfare" title="Industrial warfare">industrial war</a>", in which technological might is used to achieve military supremacy in a war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[264]</a></sup> New inventions, such as the <a href="/wiki/Military_railways" title="Military railways">train</a> and <a href="/wiki/Telegraphy" title="Telegraphy">telegraph</a>, delivered soldiers, supplies and messages at a time when horses were considered to be the fastest way to travel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271"><a href="#cite_note-271">[265]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[266]</a></sup> It was also in this war when countries first used aerial warfare, in the form of reconnaissance <a href="/wiki/History_of_aerial_warfare#American_Civil_War" title="History of aerial warfare">balloons</a>, to a significant effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273"><a href="#cite_note-273">[267]</a></sup> It saw the first action involving steam-powered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ironclad_warships" title="Ironclad warships">ironclad warships</a> in naval warfare history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274"><a href="#cite_note-274">[268]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Repeating_rifle" title="Repeating rifle">Repeating firearms</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Henry_rifle" title="Henry rifle">Henry rifle</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spencer_rifle" title="Spencer rifle">Spencer rifle</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colt_revolving_rifle" title="Colt revolving rifle">Colt revolving rifle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Triplett_%26_Scott_carbine" title="Triplett &amp; Scott carbine">Triplett &amp; Scott carbine</a> and others, first appeared during the Civil War; they were a revolutionary invention that would soon replace muzzle-loading and single-shot firearms in warfare, as well as the first appearances of rapid-firing weapons and <a href="/wiki/Machine_gun" title="Machine gun">machine guns</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Agar_gun" title="Agar gun">Agar gun</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Gatling_gun" title="Gatling gun">Gatling gun</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275">[269]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_works_of_culture_and_art">In works of culture and art</span></h2>

<p><b>Union</b></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h3>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-strength-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-strength_2-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-strength_2-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Total number that served</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Casualties-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Casualties_23-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A novel way of calculating casualties by looking at the deviation of the death rate of men of fighting age from the norm through analysis of census data found that at least 627,000 and at most 888,000 people, but most likely 761,000 people, died through the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHacker2011307.E2.80.9348_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHacker2011307.E2.80.9348-22">[21]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-UnionPop-201"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-UnionPop_201-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Union population 1864" aggregates 1860 population, average annual immigration 1855â1864, and population governed formerly by CSA per Kenneth Martis source. Contrabands and after the Emancipation Proclamation freedmen, migrating into Union control on the coasts and to the advancing armies, and natural increase are excluded.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-CSASlaves-203"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-CSASlaves_203-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Slave 1864, CSA" aggregates 1860 slave census of Virginia, North Carolina, South Carolina, Georgia and Texas. It omits losses from contraband and after the Emancipation Proclamation, freedmen migrating to the Union controlled coastal ports and those joining advancing Union armies, especially in the Mississippi Valley.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-BlacksUSATroops-237"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-BlacksUSATroops_237-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">At the beginning of the war, some Union commanders thought they were supposed to return escaped slaves to their masters. By 1862, when it became clear that this would be a long war, the question of what to do about slavery became more general. The Southern economy and military effort depended on slave labor. It began to seem unreasonable to protect slavery while blockading Southern commerce and destroying Southern production. As one Congressman put it, the slaves "...Â cannot be neutral. As laborers, if not as soldiers, they will be allies of the rebels, or of the Union."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherson1988495_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherson1988495-235">[231]</a></sup> The same Congressmanâand his fellow Radical Republicansâput pressure on Lincoln to rapidly emancipate the slaves, whereas moderate Republicans came to accept gradual, compensated emancipation and colonization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherson1988355.2C_494.E2.80.9396.2C_495_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherson1988355.2C_494.E2.80.9396.2C_495-236">[232]</a></sup> Enslaved African Americans did not wait for Lincoln's action before escaping and seeking freedom behind Union lines. From early years of the war, hundreds of thousands of African Americans escaped to Union lines, especially in occupied areas like Nashville, Norfolk and the Hampton Roads region in 1862, Tennessee from 1862 on, the line of Sherman's march, etc. So many African Americans fled to Union lines that commanders created camps and schools for them, where both adults and children learned to read and write. See Catton, Bruce. <i>Never Call Retreat</i>, p.Â 335. The American Missionary Association entered the war effort by sending teachers south to such contraband camps, for instance establishing schools in Norfolk and on nearby plantations. In addition, approximately 180,000 or more African-American men served as soldiers and sailors with Union troops. Most of those were escaped slaves. Probably the most prominent of these African-American soldiers is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/54th_Massachusetts_Volunteer_Infantry" title="54th Massachusetts Volunteer Infantry">54th Massachusetts Volunteer Infantry</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-SlavesinConfedArmy-241"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-SlavesinConfedArmy_241-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In spite of the South's shortage of soldiers, most Southern leadersâuntil 1865âopposed enlisting slaves. They used them as laborers to support the war effort. As <a href="/wiki/Howell_Cobb" title="Howell Cobb">Howell Cobb</a> said, "If slaves will make good soldiers our whole theory of slavery is wrong." Confederate generals <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Cleburne" title="Patrick Cleburne">Patrick Cleburne</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_E._Lee" title="Robert E. Lee">Robert E. Lee</a> argued in favor of arming blacks late in the war, and <a href="/wiki/Jefferson_Davis" title="Jefferson Davis">Jefferson Davis</a> was eventually persuaded to support plans for arming slaves to avoid military defeat. The Confederacy surrendered at <a href="/wiki/Appomattox,_Virginia" title="Appomattox, Virginia">Appomattox</a> before this plan could be implemented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherson1988831.E2.80.9337_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherson1988831.E2.80.9337-238">[233]</a></sup> The great majority of the 4 million slaves were freed by the Emancipation Proclamation, as Union armies moved south. Historian <a href="/wiki/John_D._Winters" title="John D. Winters">John D. Winters</a> referred to the exhilaration of the slaves when the Union Army came through <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a>: "As the troops moved up to <a href="/wiki/Alexandria,_Louisiana" title="Alexandria, Louisiana">Alexandria</a>, the Negroes crowded the roadsides to watch the passing army. They were 'all frantic with joy, some weeping, some blessing, and some dancing in the exuberance of their emotions.' All of the Negroes were attracted by the pageantry and excitement of the army. Others cheered because they anticipated the freedom to plunder and to do as they pleased now that the Federal troops were there."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinters1963237_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinters1963237-239">[234]</a></sup> Confederates enslaved captured black Union soldiers, and black soldiers especially were shot when trying to surrender at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Fort_Pillow" title="Battle of Fort Pillow">Fort Pillow Massacre</a>. See Catton, Bruce. <i>Never Call Retreat</i>, p.Â 335. This led to a breakdown of the <a href="/wiki/Postage_stamps_and_postal_history_of_the_Confederate_States#Prisoner_of_war_mail" title="Postage stamps and postal history of the Confederate States">prisoner and mail exchange program</a> and the growth of prison camps such as <a href="/wiki/Andersonville_National_Historic_Site" title="Andersonville National Historic Site">Andersonville prison</a> in Georgia, where almost 13,000 Union prisoners of war died of starvation and disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherson1988791.E2.80.9398_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherson1988791.E2.80.9398-240">[235]</a></sup></span></li>
</ol>
</div>

<li id="cite_note-StatsWarCost-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-StatsWarCost_12-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-StatsWarCost_12-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a href="/wiki/Albert_Nofi" title="Albert Nofi">Nofi, Al</a> (June 13, 2001). <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20070711050249/http://www.cwc.lsu.edu/other/stats/warcost.htm" rel="nofollow">"Statistics on the War's Costs"</a>. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">October 14,</span> 2007</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Statistics+on+the+War%27s+Costs&amp;rft.pub=Louisiana+State+University&amp;rft.date=2001-06-13&amp;rft.aulast=Nofi&amp;rft.aufirst=Al&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.cwc.lsu.edu%2Fother%2Fstats%2Fwarcost.htm&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAmerican+Civil+War"><span style="display:none;">Â </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-13">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Professor James Downs. "Color blindness in the demographic death toll of the Civil War". University of Connecticut, April 13th 2012. "The rough 19th century estimate was that 60,000 former slaves died from the epidemic, but doctors treating black patients often claimed that they were unable to keep accurate records due to demands on their time and the lack of manpower and resources. The surviving records only include the number of black patients whom doctors encountered; tens of thousands of other slaves who died had no contact with army doctors, leaving no records of their deaths." 60,000 documented plus 'tens of thousands' undocumented gives a minimum of 80,000 slave deaths.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-14">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://opinionator.blogs.nytimes.com/2011/09/20/recounting-the-dead/" rel="nofollow">Recounting the dead</a>, Associate Professor J. David Hacker, "<i>estimates, based on Census data, indicate that the [military] death toll was approximately 750,000, and may have been as high as 850,000</i>"</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Professor James Downs. "Color blindness in the demographic death toll of the Civil War". Oxford University Press, April 13th 2012. "An 2 April 2012 New York Times article, "New Estimate Raises Civil War Death Toll," reports that a new study ratchets up the death toll from an estimated 650,000 to a staggering 850,000 people. As horrific as this new number is, it fails to reflect the mortality of former slaves during the war. If former slaves were included in this figure, the Civil War death toll would likely be over a million casualtiesÂ ..."</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherson1988791.E2.80.9398-240"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherson1988791.E2.80.9398_240-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFMcPherson1988">McPherson 1988</a>, pp.Â 791â98.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-242"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-242">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Lincoln's letter to O. H. Browning, September 22, 1861. Sentiment among <a href="/wiki/German_Americans" title="German Americans">German Americans</a> was largely anti-slavery especially among <a href="/wiki/Forty-Eighters" title="Forty-Eighters">Forty-Eighters</a>, resulting in hundreds of thousands of German Americans volunteering to fight for the Union. " <cite class="citation journal">Wittke, Carl (1952). "Refugees of Revolution". Philadelphia: University of Pennsylvania press.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.atitle=Refugees+of+Revolution&amp;rft.date=1952&amp;rft.aulast=Wittke&amp;rft.aufirst=Carl&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAmerican+Civil+War"><span style="display:none;">Â </span></span> ", Christian B. Keller, "Flying Dutchmen and Drunken Irishmen: The Myths and Realities of Ethnic Civil War Soldiers", <i>Journal of Military History</i>, Vol/ 73, No. 1, January 2009, pp. 117â45; for primary sources see Walter D. Kamphoefner and Wolfgang Helbich, eds, <i>Germans in the Civil War: The Letters They Wrote Home</i> (2006). "On the other hand, many of the recent immigrants in the North viewed freed slaves as competition for scarce jobs, and as the reason why the Civil War was being fought." Baker, Kevin (March 2003). <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20101019000238/http://americanheritage.com/articles/magazine/ah/2003/1/2003_1_17.shtml" rel="nofollow">"Violent City"</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/American_Heritage_(magazine)" title="American Heritage (magazine)">American Heritage</a></i>. Retrieved July 29, 2010. "Due in large part to this fierce competition with free blacks for labor opportunities, the poor and working class <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irish_American" title="Irish American">Irish Catholics</a> generally opposed emancipation. When the draft began in the summer of 1863, they launched <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_Draft_Riots" title="New York Draft Riots">a major riot in New York City</a> that was suppressed by the military, as well as much smaller protests in other cities." Barnet Schecter, <i>The Devil's Own Work: The Civil War Draft Riots and the Fight to Reconstruct America</i> (2007), ch 6. Many Catholics in the North had volunteered to fight in 1861, sending thousands of soldiers to the front and taking high casualties, especially at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Fredericksburg" title="Battle of Fredericksburg">Fredericksburg</a>; their volunteering fell off after 1862.</span></li>
